style="text-align:left;margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><ol style="list-style-type: lower-alpha; margin-left: 1em; font-size:90%;">\n<li value="1"><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_a"><b><a href="#ref_a">^</a></b></span>  The headquarters were based from 1 November 1920 in the <a href="/wiki/Palais_Wilson" title="Palais Wilson">Palais Wilson</a> in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, Switzerland, and from 17 February 1936 in the purpose built <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Nations" title="Palace of Nations">Palace of Nations</a> also in Geneva.</li>\n</ol></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>League of Nations</b> (abbreviated as <b>LN</b> in English, <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">La Soci\xc3\xa9t\xc3\xa9 des Nations</i></i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[la s\xc9\x94sjete de n\xc9\x91sj\xc9\x94\xcc\x83]</a></span> abbreviated as <b><i lang="fr" title="French language text">SDN</i></b> or <b><i lang="fr" title="French language text">SdN</i></b> in <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>) was an <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Intergovernmental organisation">intergovernmental organisation</a> founded on 10 January 1920 as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a> that ended the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>. It was the first international organisation whose principal mission was to maintain <a href="/wiki/World_peace" title="World peace">world peace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  Its primary goals, as stated in its <a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Covenant of the League of Nations">Covenant</a>, included preventing wars through <a href="/wiki/Collective_security" title="Collective security">collective security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arms_control" title="Arms control">disarmament</a> and settling international disputes through negotiation and <a href="/wiki/Arbitration" title="Arbitration">arbitration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Other issues in this and related treaties included labour conditions, just treatment of native inhabitants, human and drug trafficking, the arms trade, global health, prisoners of war, and protection of minorities in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> At its greatest extent from 28 September 1934 to 23 February 1935, it had 58 members.\n</p><p>The diplomatic philosophy behind the League represented a fundamental shift from the preceding hundred years. The League lacked its own armed force and depended on the victorious <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Great Powers of World War I</a> (France, the United Kingdom, Italy and Japan were the permanent members of the executive Council) to enforce its resolutions, keep to its economic sanctions, or provide an army when needed. The Great Powers were often reluctant to do so. Sanctions could hurt League members, so they were reluctant to comply with them. During the <a href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War">Second Italo-Abyssinian War</a>, when the League accused Italian soldiers of targeting <a href="/wiki/Red_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Cross">Red Cross</a> medical tents, <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> responded that "the League is very well when sparrows shout, but no good at all when eagles fall out."<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After some notable successes and some early failures in the 1920s, the League ultimately proved incapable of preventing aggression by the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a> in the 1930s. The credibility of the organization was weakened by the fact that the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> never officially joined the League and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> joined late and only briefly.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Germany withdrew from the League, as did Japan, Italy, Spain and others. The onset of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> showed that the League had failed its primary purpose, which was to prevent any future world war. The League lasted for 26 years; the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (UN) replaced it after the end of the Second World War and inherited several agencies and organisations founded by the League.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" class="toctext">Members</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Mandates"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Mandates</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Mandatory_powers"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Mandatory powers</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Resolving_territorial_disputes"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Resolving territorial disputes</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#\xc3\x85land_Islands"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">\xc3\x85land Islands</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Upper_Silesia"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Upper Silesia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Albania"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Albania</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Memel"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Memel</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Hatay"><span class="tocnumber">6.5</span> <span class="toctext">Hatay</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Mosul"><span class="tocnumber">6.6</span> <span class="toctext">Mosul</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Vilnius"><span class="tocnumber">6.7</span> <span class="toctext">Vilnius</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Colombia_and_Peru"><span class="tocnumber">6.8</span> <span class="toctext">Colombia and Peru</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Saar"><span class="tocnumber">6.9</span> <span class="toctext">Saar</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Other_conflicts"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Other conflicts</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Greece_and_Bulgaria"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Greece and Bulgaria</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Liberia"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Liberia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Mukden_Incident:_Japan_attacks_China"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Mukden Incident: Japan attacks China</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Chaco_War"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Chaco War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Italian_invasion_of_Abyssinia"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">Italian invasion of Abyssinia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Spanish_Civil_War"><span class="tocnumber">7.6</span> <span class="toctext">Spanish Civil War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Second_Sino-Japanese_War"><span class="tocnumber">7.7</span> <span class="toctext">Second Sino-Japanese War</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Failure_of_disarmament"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Failure of disarmament</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#General_weaknesses"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">General weaknesses</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Origins_and_structure"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Origins and structure</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Global_representation"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Global representation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Collective_security"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Collective security</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Pacifism_and_disarmament"><span class="tocnumber">9.4</span> <span class="toctext">Pacifism and disarmament</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Demise_and_legacy"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Demise and legacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Surveys"><span class="tocnumber">12.3</span> <span class="toctext">Surveys</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">12.4</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#League_topics"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">League topics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#Specialized_topics"><span class="tocnumber">13.1</span> <span class="toctext">Specialized topics</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg/220px-Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg/330px-Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg/440px-Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1882" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/First_Geneva_Convention" title="First Geneva Convention">1864 Geneva Convention</a>, one of the earliest formulations of <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The concept of a peaceful community of nations had been proposed as far back as 1795, when <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Perpetual_Peace:_A_Philosophical_Sketch" title="Perpetual Peace: A Philosophical Sketch">Perpetual Peace: A Philosophical Sketch</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> outlined the idea of a league of nations to control conflict and promote peace between states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkirbekkGilje2001288_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkirbekkGilje2001288-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Kant argued for the establishment of a peaceful world community, not in a sense of a global government, but in the hope that each state would declare itself a free state that respects its citizens and welcomes foreign visitors as fellow rational beings, thus promoting peaceful society worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> International co-operation to promote collective security originated in the <a href="/wiki/Concert_of_Europe" title="Concert of Europe">Concert of Europe</a> that developed after the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> in the 19th century in an attempt to maintain the <i>status quo</i> between European states and so avoid war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReichard20069_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReichard20069-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERapoport1995498\xe2\x80\x93500_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERapoport1995498\xe2\x80\x93500-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> This period also saw the development of international law, with the first <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conventions" title="Geneva Conventions">Geneva Conventions</a> establishing laws dealing with humanitarian relief during wartime, and the international <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_of_1899_and_1907" title="Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907">Hague Conventions of 1899 and 1907</a> governing rules of war and the peaceful settlement of international disputes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBouchet-SaulnierBravOlivier200714\xe2\x80\x93134_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBouchet-SaulnierBravOlivier200714\xe2\x80\x93134-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> As historians William H. Harbaugh and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_E._Powaski" title="Ronald E. Powaski">Ronald E. Powaski</a> point out, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">American President</a> to call for an international league.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> At the acceptance for his Nobel Prize, Roosevelt said: "it would be a masterstroke if those great powers honestly bent on peace would form a League of Peace."<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The forerunner of the League of Nations, the <a href="/wiki/Inter-Parliamentary_Union" title="Inter-Parliamentary Union">Inter-Parliamentary Union</a> (IPU), was formed by the peace activists <a href="/wiki/William_Randal_Cremer" class="mw-redirect" title="William Randal Cremer">William Randal Cremer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Passy" title="Fr\xc3\xa9d\xc3\xa9ric Passy">Fr\xc3\xa9d\xc3\xa9ric Passy</a> in 1889 (and is currently still in existence as an international body with a focus on the various elected legislative bodies of the world.)  The IPU was founded with an international scope, with a third of the members of <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">parliaments</a> (in the 24 countries that had parliaments) serving as members of the IPU by 1914. Its foundational aims were to encourage governments to solve international disputes by peaceful means. Annual conferences were established to help governments refine the process of international arbitration. Its structure was designed as a council headed by a president, which would later be reflected in the structure of the League.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_proposals">Initial proposals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Initial proposals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1st_Viscount_Bryce_1893.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/1st_Viscount_Bryce_1893.jpg/170px-1st_Viscount_Bryce_1893.jpg" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/1st_Viscount_Bryce_1893.jpg/255px-1st_Viscount_Bryce_1893.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/1st_Viscount_Bryce_1893.jpg 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="394" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1st_Viscount_Bryce_1893.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Bryce,_1st_Viscount_Bryce" title="James Bryce, 1st Viscount Bryce">Lord Bryce</a>, one of the earliest advocates for a League of Nations.</div></div></div>\n<p>At the start of the First World War the first schemes for international organisation to prevent future wars began to gain considerable public support, particularly in Great Britain and the United States. <a href="/wiki/Goldsworthy_Lowes_Dickinson" title="Goldsworthy Lowes Dickinson">Goldsworthy Lowes Dickinson</a>, a British political scientist, coined the term "League of Nations" in 1914 and drafted a scheme for its organisation. Together with <a href="/wiki/James_Bryce,_1st_Viscount_Bryce" title="James Bryce, 1st Viscount Bryce">Lord Bryce</a>, he played a leading role in the founding of the group of internationalist pacifists known as the Bryce Group, later the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_Union" title="League of Nations Union">League of Nations Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-northedge_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northedge-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The group became steadily more influential among the public and as a pressure group within the then governing <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>. In Dickinson\'s 1915 pamphlet <i>After the War</i> he wrote of his "League of Peace" as being essentially an organisation for arbitration and conciliation. He felt that the secret diplomacy of the early twentieth century had brought about war and thus could write that, "the impossibility of war, I believe, would be increased in proportion as the issues of foreign policy should be known to and controlled by public opinion." The \xe2\x80\x98Proposals\xe2\x80\x99 of the Bryce Group were circulated widely, both in England and the US, where they had a profound influence on the nascent international movement.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Within two weeks of the start of the war, feminists began to mobilise against the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarchand2015182-184_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarchand2015182-184-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Having been barred from participating in prior peace organizations,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarchand2015102_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarchand2015102-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> American women formed a Women\'s Peace Parade Committee to plan a silent protest to the war. Led by chairwoman <a href="/wiki/Fanny_Garrison_Villard" title="Fanny Garrison Villard">Fanny Garrison Villard</a>, women from trade unions, feminist organizations, and social reform organizations, such as <a href="/wiki/Kate_Waller_Barrett" title="Kate Waller Barrett">Kate Waller Barrett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Ritter_Beard" title="Mary Ritter Beard">Mary Ritter Beard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carrie_Chapman_Catt" title="Carrie Chapman Catt">Carrie Chapman Catt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rose_Schneiderman" title="Rose Schneiderman">Rose Schneiderman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lillian_Wald" title="Lillian Wald">Lillian Wald</a>, and others, organized 1500 women, who marched down <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue" title="Fifth Avenue">Fifth Avenue</a> on August 29, 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarchand2015182-184_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarchand2015182-184-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> As a result of the parade, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Addams" title="Jane Addams">Jane Addams</a> became interested in proposals by two European suffragists\xe2\x80\x94Hungarian <a href="/wiki/Rosika_Schwimmer" title="Rosika Schwimmer">Rosika Schwimmer</a> and British <a href="/wiki/Emmeline_Pethick-Lawrence,_Baroness_Pethick-Lawrence" title="Emmeline Pethick-Lawrence, Baroness Pethick-Lawrence">Emmeline Pethick-Lawrence</a>\xe2\x80\x94to hold a peace conference.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarchand2015194_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarchand2015194-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> On 9\xe2\x80\x9310 January 1915, a peace conference directed by Addams was held in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D._C." class="mw-redirect" title="Washington, D. C.">Washington, D. C.</a>, where the delegates adopted a platform calling for creation of international bodies with administrative and legislative powers to develop a "permanent league of neutral nations" to work for peace and disarmament.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Washington_Herald_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Washington_Herald-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Within months a call was made for an international women\'s conference to be held in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a>. Coordinated by <a href="/wiki/Mia_Boissevain" title="Mia Boissevain">Mia Boissevain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aletta_Jacobs" title="Aletta Jacobs">Aletta Jacobs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rosa_Manus" title="Rosa Manus">Rosa Manus</a>, the Congress, which opened on April 28, 1915<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEverardde_Haan201664-65_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverardde_Haan201664-65-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> was attended by 1,136 participants from both neutral and non-<a href="/wiki/Belligerent" title="Belligerent">belligerent</a> <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nations</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> and resulted in the establishment of an organization which would become the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_International_League_for_Peace_and_Freedom" title="Women&#39;s International League for Peace and Freedom">Women\'s International League for Peace and Freedom</a> (WILPF).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobs199694_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobs199694-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> At the close of the conference, two delegations of women were dispatched to meet European heads of state over the next several months. They secured agreement from reluctant Foreign Ministers, who overall felt that such a body would be ineffective, but agreed to participate or not impede creation of a neutral mediating body, if other nations agreed and if President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> would initiate a body. In the midst of the War, Wilson refused.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECaravantes2004101-103_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECaravantes2004101-103-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiltsher1985110-125_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiltsher1985110-125-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1915, a similar body to the Bryce group proposals was set up in the United States by a group of like-minded individuals, including <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a>. It was called the <a href="/wiki/League_to_Enforce_Peace" title="League to Enforce Peace">League to Enforce Peace</a> and was substantially based on the proposals of the Bryce Group.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> It advocated the use of arbitration in conflict resolution and the imposition of sanctions on aggressive countries. None of these early organisations envisioned a continuously functioning body; with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian Society</a> in England, they maintained a legalistic approach that would limit the international body to a court of justice. The Fabians were the first to argue for a "Council" of states, necessarily the <a href="/wiki/Great_Power" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Power">Great Powers</a>, who would adjudicate world affairs, and for the creation of a permanent secretariat to enhance international co-operation across a range of activities.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the course of the <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_history_of_World_War_I" title="Diplomatic history of World War I">diplomatic efforts surrounding World War I</a>, both sides had to clarify their long-term war aims. By 1916 in Britain, the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a>, and in neutral United States, long-range thinkers had begun to design a unified international organisation to prevent future wars. Historian Peter Yearwood argues that when the new coalition government of <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_George_ministry" title="Lloyd George ministry">David Lloyd George</a> took power in December 1916, there was widespread discussion among intellectuals and diplomats of the desirability of establishing such an organisation, when Lloyd George was challenged by Wilson to state his position With an eye on the postwar situation, he endorsed such an organisation.  Wilson himself included in his <a href="/wiki/Fourteen_Points" title="Fourteen Points">Fourteen Points</a> in January 1918 a "league of nations to insure peace and justice." British foreign secretary, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, argued that, as a condition of durable peace, "behind international law, and behind all treaty arrangements for preventing or limiting hostilities, some form of international sanction should be devised which would give pause to the hardiest aggressor."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The war had had a profound impact, affecting the social, political and economic systems of Europe and inflicting psychological and physical damage.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Several empires collapsed: first the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> in February 1917, followed by the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Austro-Hungarian Empire">Austro-Hungarian Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.  Anti-war sentiment rose across the world; the First World War was described as "<a href="/wiki/The_war_to_end_war" title="The war to end war">the war to end all wars</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArcher200114_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArcher200114-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> and its possible causes were vigorously investigated. The causes identified included arms races, alliances, militaristic nationalism, secret diplomacy, and the freedom of sovereign states to enter into war for their own benefit. One proposed remedy was the creation of an international organisation whose aim was to prevent future war through disarmament, open diplomacy, international co-operation, restrictions on the right to wage war, and penalties that made war unattractive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell20078_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell20078-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In London Balfour commissioned the first official report into the matter in early 1918, under the initiative of Lord <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_1st_Viscount_Cecil_of_Chelwood" title="Robert Cecil, 1st Viscount Cecil of Chelwood">Robert Cecil</a>. The British committee was finally appointed in February 1918. It was led by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Phillimore" class="mw-redirect" title="Walter Phillimore">Walter Phillimore</a> (and became known as the Phillimore Committee), but also included <a href="/wiki/Eyre_Crowe" title="Eyre Crowe">Eyre Crowe</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Tyrrell,_1st_Baron_Tyrrell" title="William Tyrrell, 1st Baron Tyrrell">William Tyrrell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Hurst" title="Cecil Hurst">Cecil Hurst</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-northedge_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northedge-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The recommendations of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Walter_Phillimore" class="mw-redirect" title="Walter Phillimore">Phillimore Commission</a> included the establishment of a "Conference of Allied States" that would arbitrate disputes and impose sanctions on offending states. The proposals were approved by the British government, and much of the commission\'s results were later incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Covenant of the League of Nations">Covenant of the League of Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jclare_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jclare-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JanSmutsFM.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/JanSmutsFM.png/170px-JanSmutsFM.png" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/JanSmutsFM.png/255px-JanSmutsFM.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/JanSmutsFM.png 2x" data-file-width="295" data-file-height="350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JanSmutsFM.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jan_Smuts" title="Jan Smuts">Jan Smuts</a> helped to draft the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Covenant of the League of Nations">Covenant of the League of Nations</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The French also drafted a much more far-reaching proposal in June 1918; they advocated annual meetings of a council to settle all disputes, as well as an "international army" to enforce its decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-jclare_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jclare-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The American President Woodrow Wilson instructed <a href="/wiki/Edward_M._House" title="Edward M. House">Edward M. House</a> to draft a US plan which reflected Wilson\'s own idealistic views (first articulated in the <a href="/wiki/Fourteen_Points" title="Fourteen Points">Fourteen Points</a> of January 1918), as well as the work of the Phillimore Commission. The outcome of House\'s work, and Wilson\'s own first draft, proposed the termination of "unethical" state behaviour, including forms of espionage and dishonesty. Methods of compulsion against recalcitrant states would include severe measures, such as "blockading and closing the frontiers of that power to commerce or intercourse with any part of the world and to use any force that may be necessary..."<sup id="cite_ref-jclare_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jclare-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The two principal drafters and architects of the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Covenant of the League of Nations">covenant of the League of Nations</a><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> were the British politician Lord <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_1st_Viscount_Cecil_of_Chelwood" title="Robert Cecil, 1st Viscount Cecil of Chelwood">Robert Cecil</a> and the South African statesman <a href="/wiki/Jan_Smuts" title="Jan Smuts">Jan Smuts</a>. Smuts\' proposals included the creation of a Council of the great powers as permanent members and a non-permanent selection of the minor states. He also proposed the creation of a <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_Mandate" class="mw-redirect" title="League of Nations Mandate">Mandate</a> system for captured colonies of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a> during the war. Cecil focused on the administrative side, and proposed annual Council meetings and quadrennial meetings for the Assembly of all members. He also argued for a large and permanent secretariat to carry out the League\'s administrative duties.<sup id="cite_ref-jclare_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jclare-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment">Establishment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Establishment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:No-nb_bldsa_5c006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/No-nb_bldsa_5c006.jpg/220px-No-nb_bldsa_5c006.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/No-nb_bldsa_5c006.jpg/330px-No-nb_bldsa_5c006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/No-nb_bldsa_5c006.jpg/440px-No-nb_bldsa_5c006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5622" data-file-height="4281" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:No-nb_bldsa_5c006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The official opening of the League of Nations, 15 November 1920</div></div></div>\n<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a> in 1919, Wilson, Cecil and Smuts all put forward their draft proposals. After lengthy negotiations between the delegates, the <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Hurst" title="Cecil Hurst">Hurst</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/David_Hunter_Miller" title="David Hunter Miller">Miller</a> draft was finally produced as a basis for the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Covenant of the League of Nations">Covenant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> After more negotiation and compromise, the delegates finally approved of the proposal to create the League of Nations (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">Soci\xc3\xa9t\xc3\xa9 des Nations</i>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">V\xc3\xb6lkerbund</i>) on 25 January 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagliveras19998_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagliveras19998-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The final <a href="/wiki/Covenant_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Covenant of the League of Nations">Covenant of the League of Nations</a> was drafted by a special commission, and the League was established by Part I of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>. On 28 June 1919,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagliveras19998\xe2\x80\x9312_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagliveras19998\xe2\x80\x9312-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198635\xe2\x80\x9336_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198635\xe2\x80\x9336-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> 44 states signed the Covenant, including 31 states which had taken part in the war on the side of the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Entente" title="Triple Entente">Triple Entente</a> or joined it during the conflict.\n</p><p>French women\'s rights advocates invited international feminists to participate in a parallel conference to the Paris Conference in hopes that they could gain permission to participate in the official conference.<sup id="cite_ref-Sydney_Herald_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sydney_Herald-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The Inter-Allied Women\'s Conference asked to be allowed to submit suggestions to the peace negotiations and commissions and were granted the right to sit on commissions dealing specifically with women and children.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Though they asked for enfranchisement and full legal protection under the law equal with men,<sup id="cite_ref-Sydney_Herald_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sydney_Herald-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> those rights were ignored.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiltsher1985200-202_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiltsher1985200-202-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Women won the right to serve in all capacities, including as staff or delegates in the League of Nations organization.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyerPr\xc3\xbcgl199920_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyerPr\xc3\xbcgl199920-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> They also won a declaration that member nations should prevent <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">trafficking</a> of women and children and should equally support humane conditions for children, women and men labourers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietil\xc3\xa419992_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietil\xc3\xa419992-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Z%C3%BCrich" title="Z\xc3\xbcrich">Z\xc3\xbcrich</a> Peace Conference held between 17\xe2\x80\x9319 May 1919, the women of the WILPF condemned the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> for both is punitive measures, as well as its failure to provide for condemnation of violence and exclusion of women from civil and political participation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiltsher1985200-202_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiltsher1985200-202-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Upon reading the Rules of Procedure for the League of Nations, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Marshall_(suffragette)" class="mw-redirect" title="Catherine Marshall (suffragette)">Catherine Marshall</a>, a British suffragist, discovered that the guidelines were completely undemocratic and they were modified based on her suggestion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiltsher1985212_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiltsher1985212-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The League would be made up of a General Assembly (representing all member states), an Executive Council (with membership limited to major powers), and a permanent secretariat. Member states were expected to "respect and preserve as against external aggression" the territorial integrity of other members and to <a href="/wiki/Disarmament" title="Disarmament">disarm</a> "to the lowest point consistent with domestic safety." All states were required to submit complaints for <a href="/wiki/Arbitration" title="Arbitration">arbitration</a> or <a href="/wiki/Judicial_inquiry" class="mw-redirect" title="Judicial inquiry">judicial inquiry</a> before going to war.<sup id="cite_ref-northedge_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-northedge-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The Executive Council would create a <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_International_Justice" title="Permanent Court of International Justice">Permanent Court of International Justice</a> to make judgements on the disputes.\n</p><p>Despite Wilson\'s efforts to establish and promote the League, for which he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> in October 1919,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevinovitzRingertz2001170_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevinovitzRingertz2001170-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> the United States never joined. Senate Republicans led by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge" title="Henry Cabot Lodge">Henry Cabot Lodge</a> wanted a League with the reservation that only Congress could take the U.S. into war. Lodge gained a majority of Senators. Wilson refused to allow a compromise and the needed 2/3 majority was lacking.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The League held its first council meeting in Paris on 16 January 1920, six days after the Versailles Treaty and the Covenant of the League of Nations came into force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197351_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197351-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> On 1 November 1920, the headquarters of the League was moved from London to <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a>, where the first General Assembly was held on 15 November 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197367_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197367-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Palais_Wilson" title="Palais Wilson">Palais Wilson</a> on Geneva\'s western lakeshore, named after US President Woodrow Wilson in recognition of his efforts towards the establishment of the League, was the League\'s first permanent home.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages_and_symbols">Languages and symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Languages and symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_League_of_Nations_(1939).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Flag_of_the_League_of_Nations_%281939%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_League_of_Nations_%281939%29.svg.png" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Flag_of_the_League_of_Nations_%281939%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_League_of_Nations_%281939%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Flag_of_the_League_of_Nations_%281939%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_League_of_Nations_%281939%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="951" data-file-height="633" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_League_of_Nations_(1939).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Semi-official flag of the League of Nations from 1939 to 1941.</div></div></div><p>The official languages of the League of Nations were French and English.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeague_of_Nations193522_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeague_of_Nations193522-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The League rejected adopting <a href="/wiki/Esperanto" title="Esperanto">Esperanto</a> as its working language. China and Japan wanted Esperanto but France was strongly opposed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForster1982171\xe2\x80\x9376_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForster1982171\xe2\x80\x9376-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1939, a semi-official emblem for the League of Nations emerged: two five-pointed stars within a blue pentagon. They symbolised the Earth\'s five continents and five "<a href="/wiki/Historical_race_concepts" title="Historical race concepts">races</a>". A bow at the top displayed the English name ("League of Nations"), while another at the bottom showed the French ("<i>Soci\xc3\xa9t\xc3\xa9 des Nations</i>").<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Principal_organs">Principal organs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Principal organs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Organisation of the League of Nations">Organisation of the League of Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_International_Justice" title="Permanent Court of International Justice">Permanent Court of International Justice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leaders_of_the_League_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaders of the League of Nations">Leaders of the League of Nations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:League_of_Nations_Organization.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/League_of_Nations_Organization.png/220px-League_of_Nations_Organization.png" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/League_of_Nations_Organization.png/330px-League_of_Nations_Organization.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/League_of_Nations_Organization.png/440px-League_of_Nations_Organization.png 2x" data-file-width="2437" data-file-height="3446" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:League_of_Nations_Organization.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>League of Nations Organisation chart<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Palais_des_nations.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A drive leads past a manicured lawn to large white rectangular building with columns on it facade. Two wings of the building are set back from the middle section." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Palais_des_nations.jpg/220px-Palais_des_nations.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Palais_des_nations.jpg/330px-Palais_des_nations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Palais_des_nations.jpg/440px-Palais_des_nations.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1550" data-file-height="1160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Palais_des_nations.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Nations" title="Palace of Nations">Palace of Nations</a>, Geneva, the League\'s headquarters from 1936 until its dissolution in 1946</div></div></div>\n<p>The main constitutional organs of the League were the Assembly, the Council, and the Permanent Secretariat. It also had two essential wings: the Permanent Court of International Justice and the <a href="/wiki/International_Labour_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="International Labour Organisation">International Labour Organisation</a>. In addition, there were several auxiliary agencies and commissions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198648,_66_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198648,_66-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Each organ\'s budget was allocated by the Assembly (the League was supported financially by its member states).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The relations between the Assembly and the Council and the competencies of each were for the most part not explicitly defined. Each body could deal with any matter within the sphere of competence of the League or affecting peace in the world. Particular questions or tasks might be referred to either.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198648\xe2\x80\x9349_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198648\xe2\x80\x9349-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unanimity was required for the decisions of both the Assembly and the Council, except in matters of procedure and some other specific cases such as the admission of new members. This requirement was a reflection of the League\'s belief in the sovereignty of its component nations; the League sought solution by consent, not by dictation. In case of a dispute, the consent of the parties to the dispute was not required for unanimity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198653_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198653-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Permanent Secretariat, established at the seat of the League at Geneva, comprised a body of experts in various spheres under the direction of the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_General" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary General">general secretary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198650_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198650-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Its principal sections were Political, Financial and Economics, Transit, Minorities and Administration (administering the <a href="/wiki/Saar_(League_of_Nations)" class="mw-redirect" title="Saar (League of Nations)">Saar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gda\xc5\x84sk">Danzig</a>), Mandates, Disarmament, Health, Social (Opium and Traffic in Women and Children), Intellectual Cooperation and International Bureaux, Legal, and Information. The staff of the Secretariat was responsible for preparing the agenda for the Council and the Assembly and publishing reports of the meetings and other routine matters, effectively acting as the League\'s civil service. In 1931 the staff numbered 707.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Assembly consisted of representatives of all members of the League, with each state allowed up to three representatives and one vote.<sup id="cite_ref-UNOG_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNOG-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> It met in Geneva and, after its initial sessions in 1920,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198672_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198672-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> it convened once a year in September.<sup id="cite_ref-UNOG_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNOG-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The special functions of the Assembly included the admission of new members, the periodical election of non-permanent members to the Council, the election with the Council of the judges of the Permanent Court, and control of the budget. In practice, the Assembly was the general directing force of League activities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198648\xe2\x80\x9350_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198648\xe2\x80\x9350-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The League Council acted as a type of executive body directing the Assembly\'s business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198648_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198648-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> It began with four permanent members (<a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Great Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>) and four non-permanent members that were elected by the Assembly for a three-year term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198642\xe2\x80\x9348_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198642\xe2\x80\x9348-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The first non-permanent members were <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-photo_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-photo-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The composition of the Council was changed several times. The number of non-permanent members was first increased to six on 22 September 1922 and to nine on 8 September 1926. <a href="/wiki/Werner_Dankwort" title="Werner Dankwort">Werner Dankwort</a> of Germany pushed for his country to join the League; joining in 1926, Germany became the fifth permanent member of the Council. Later, after Germany and Japan both left the League, the number of non-permanent seats was increased from nine to eleven, and the Soviet Union was made a permanent member giving the Council a total of fifteen members.<sup id="cite_ref-photo_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-photo-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The Council met, on average, five times a year and in extraordinary sessions when required. In total, 107 sessions were held between 1920 and 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_bodies">Other bodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Other bodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The League oversaw the Permanent Court of International Justice and several other agencies and commissions created to deal with pressing international problems. These included the Disarmament Commission, the International Labour Organisation (ILO), the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">Mandates</a> Commission, the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_on_Intellectual_Cooperation" title="International Committee on Intellectual Cooperation">International Commission on Intellectual Cooperation</a><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> (precursor to <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>), the Permanent Central Opium Board, the Commission for Refugees, and the Slavery Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Three of these institutions were transferred to the United Nations after the Second World War: the International Labour Organisation, the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_International_Justice" title="Permanent Court of International Justice">Permanent Court of International Justice</a> (as the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">Health Organisation</a><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> (restructured as the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="World Health Organisation">World Health Organisation</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Permanent Court of International Justice was provided for by the Covenant, but not established by it. The Council and the Assembly established its constitution. Its judges were elected by the Council and the Assembly, and its budget was provided by the latter. The Court was to hear and decide any international dispute which the parties concerned submitted to it. It might also give an advisory opinion on any dispute or question referred to it by the Council or the Assembly. The Court was open to all the nations of the world under certain broad conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Childlabourcoal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Childlabourcoal.jpg/220px-Childlabourcoal.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Childlabourcoal.jpg/330px-Childlabourcoal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Childlabourcoal.jpg/440px-Childlabourcoal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="401" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Childlabourcoal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Child labour in a coal mine, United States, c. 1912</div></div></div>\n<p>The International Labour Organisation was created in 1919 on the basis of Part XIII of the Treaty of Versailles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986179\xe2\x80\x9380_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986179\xe2\x80\x9380-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The ILO, although having the same members as the League and being subject to the budget control of the Assembly, was an autonomous organisation with its own Governing Body, its own General Conference and its own Secretariat. Its constitution differed from that of the League: representation had been accorded not only to governments but also to representatives of employers\' and workers\' organisations. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Thomas_(minister)" title="Albert Thomas (minister)">Albert Thomas</a> was its first director.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197353_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197353-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kamerun_children_weaving.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A row of more than a dozen children holding wooden looms stretches into the distance." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Kamerun_children_weaving.jpg/220px-Kamerun_children_weaving.jpg" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Kamerun_children_weaving.jpg/330px-Kamerun_children_weaving.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Kamerun_children_weaving.jpg/440px-Kamerun_children_weaving.jpg 2x" data-file-width="758" data-file-height="470" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kamerun_children_weaving.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Child labour in <a href="/wiki/Kamerun" title="Kamerun">Kamerun</a> in 1919</div></div></div>\n<p>The ILO successfully restricted the addition of lead to paint,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFroweinR\xc3\xbcdiger2000167_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFroweinR\xc3\xbcdiger2000167-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> and convinced several countries to adopt an <a href="/wiki/Eight-hour_day" title="Eight-hour day">eight-hour work day</a> and forty-eight-hour working week. It also campaigned to end child labour, increase the rights of women in the workplace, and make shipowners liable for accidents involving seamen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986179\xe2\x80\x9380_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986179\xe2\x80\x9380-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> After the demise of the League, the ILO became an agency of the United Nations in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The League\'s health organisation had three bodies: the Health Bureau, containing permanent officials of the League; the General Advisory Council or Conference, an executive section consisting of medical experts; and the Health Committee. The Committee\'s purpose was to conduct inquiries, oversee the operation of the League\'s health work, and prepare work to be presented to the Council.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986182_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986182-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> This body focused on ending <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>, the latter two by starting an international campaign to exterminate mosquitoes. The Health Organisation also worked successfully with the government of the Soviet Union to prevent <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a> epidemics, including organising a large education campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaumslag20058_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaumslag20058-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The League of Nations had devoted serious attention to the question of international intellectual co-operation since its creation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrandjean2018_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrandjean2018-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The First Assembly in December 1920 recommended that the Council take action aiming at the international organisation of intellectual work, which it did by adopting a report presented by the Fifth Committee of the Second Assembly and inviting a Committee on Intellectual Cooperation to meet in Geneva in August 1922. The French philosopher <a href="/wiki/Henri_Bergson" title="Henri Bergson">Henri Bergson</a> became the first chairman of the committee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986186\xe2\x80\x93187_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986186\xe2\x80\x93187-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The work of the committee included: inquiry into the conditions of intellectual life, assistance to countries where intellectual life was endangered, creation of national committees for intellectual co-operation, co-operation with international intellectual organisations, protection of intellectual property, inter-university co-operation, co-ordination of bibliographical work and international interchange of publications, and international co-operation in archaeological research.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986187\xe2\x80\x93189_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986187\xe2\x80\x93189-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Introduced by the second <a href="/wiki/International_Opium_Convention" title="International Opium Convention">International Opium Convention</a>, the Permanent Central Opium Board had to supervise the statistical reports on trade in <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a>, morphine, cocaine and heroin. The board also established a system of import certificates and export authorisations for the legal international trade in <a href="/wiki/Narcotics" class="mw-redirect" title="Narcotics">narcotics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcAllister199976\xe2\x80\x9377_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcAllister199976\xe2\x80\x9377-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/1926_Slavery_Convention" title="1926 Slavery Convention">Slavery Commission</a> sought to eradicate <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> and slave trading across the world, and fought forced prostitution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986185\xe2\x80\x9386_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986185\xe2\x80\x9386-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Its main success was through pressing the governments who administered mandated countries to end slavery in those countries. The League secured a commitment from <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> to end slavery as a condition of membership in 1923, and worked with <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> to abolish forced labour and intertribal slavery. The United Kingdom had not supported Ethiopian membership of the League on the grounds that "Ethiopia had not reached a state of civilisation and internal security sufficient to warrant her admission."<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986185\xe2\x80\x9386_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986185\xe2\x80\x9386-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The League also succeeded in reducing the death rate of workers constructing the <a href="/wiki/Usambara_Railway" title="Usambara Railway">Tanganyika railway</a> from 55 to 4 percent. Records were kept to control slavery, prostitution, and the <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">trafficking of women and children</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986166_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986166-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Partly as a result of pressure brought by the League of Nations, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> abolished slavery in 1923, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> in 1924, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> in 1926, <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Transjordan" title="Emirate of Transjordan">Transjordan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persia" class="mw-redirect" title="Persia">Persia</a> in 1929, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a> in 1937, and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nansenpassport.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Nansenpassport.jpg/170px-Nansenpassport.jpg" width="170" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/Nansenpassport.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="255" data-file-height="375" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nansenpassport.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sample <a href="/wiki/Nansen_passport" title="Nansen passport">Nansen passport</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Led by <a href="/wiki/Fridtjof_Nansen" title="Fridtjof Nansen">Fridtjof Nansen</a>, the Commission for Refugees was established on 27 June 1921<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> to look after the interests of refugees, including overseeing their <a href="/wiki/Repatriation" title="Repatriation">repatriation</a> and, when necessary, resettlement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198677_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198677-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the First World War, there were two to three million ex-prisoners of war from various nations dispersed throughout Russia;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198677_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198677-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> within two years of the commission\'s foundation, it had helped 425,000 of them return home.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197359_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197359-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> It established camps in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> in 1922 to aid the country with an ongoing refugee crisis, helping to prevent disease and hunger. It also established the <a href="/wiki/Nansen_passport" title="Nansen passport">Nansen passport</a> as a means of identification for <a href="/wiki/Stateless_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Stateless people">stateless people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorpey2000129_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorpey2000129-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Committee for the Study of the Legal Status of Women sought to inquire into the status of women all over the world. It was formed in 1937, and later became part of the United Nations as the Commission on the Status of Women.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Members">Members</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Members">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Member states of the League of Nations">Member states of the League of Nations</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LN_member_states_animation.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/LN_member_states_animation.gif/350px-LN_member_states_animation.gif" width="350" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/LN_member_states_animation.gif/525px-LN_member_states_animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/LN_member_states_animation.gif/700px-LN_member_states_animation.gif 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LN_member_states_animation.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the world in 1920\xe2\x80\x9345, which shows the League of Nations members during its history</div></div></div>\n<p>Of the League\'s 42 founding members, 23 (24 counting <a href="/wiki/Free_French_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Free French Forces">Free France</a>) remained members until it was dissolved in 1946. In the founding year, six other states joined, only two of which remained members throughout the League\'s existence. The <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a>\n</p><p>An additional 15 countries joined later. The largest number of member states was 58, between 28 September 1934 (when <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a> joined) and 23 February 1935 (when <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> withdrew).<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 26 May 1937, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Kingdom of Egypt">Egypt</a> became the last state to join the League. The first member to withdraw permanently from the League was <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> on 22 January 1925; having joined on 16 December 1920, this also makes it the member to have most quickly withdrawn. Brazil was the first founding member to withdraw (14 June 1926), and <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> the last (April 1942). <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, which joined in 1932, was the first member that had previously been a <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">League of Nations mandate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripp200275_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripp200275-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Soviet Union became a member on 18 September 1934,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973312,_398_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973312,_398-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> and was expelled on 14 December 1939<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973312,_398_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973312,_398-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> for <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">invading Finland</a>. In expelling the Soviet Union, the League broke its own rule: only 7 of 15 members of the Council voted for expulsion (United Kingdom, France, Belgium, Bolivia, Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">South Africa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>), short of the majority required by the Covenant. Three of these members had been made Council members the day before the vote (South Africa, Bolivia, and Egypt). This was one of the League\'s final acts before it practically ceased functioning due to the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagliveras199931_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagliveras199931-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mandates">Mandates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mandates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">League of Nations mandate</a></div>\n<p>At the end of the First World War, the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied powers</a> were confronted with the question of the disposal of the former German colonies in Africa and the Pacific, and the several Arabic-speaking provinces of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Peace Conference</a> adopted the principle that these territories should be administered by different governments on behalf of the League \xe2\x80\x93 a system of national responsibility subject to international supervision.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986192\xe2\x80\x93193_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986192\xe2\x80\x93193-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> This plan, defined as the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">mandate system</a>, was adopted by the "Council of Ten" (the heads of government and foreign ministers of the main Allied powers: Britain, France, the United States, Italy, and Japan) on 30 January 1919 and transmitted to the League of Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>League of Nations mandates were established under Article 22 of the Covenant of the League of Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986193_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986193-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The Permanent Mandates Commission supervised League of Nations mandates,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986198_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986198-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> and also organised <a href="/wiki/Plebiscite" class="mw-redirect" title="Plebiscite">plebiscites</a> in disputed territories so that residents could decide which country they would join. There were three mandate classifications: A, B and C.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986195_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986195-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nThe A mandates (applied to parts of the old Ottoman Empire) were "certain communities" that had </p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...reached a stage of development where their existence as independent nations can be provisionally recognised subject to the rendering of administrative advice and assistance by a Mandatory until such time as they are able to stand alone. The wishes of these communities must be a principal consideration in the selection of the Mandatory.<sup id="cite_ref-Mandate_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandate-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Article 22, The Covenant of the League of Nations</cite></div>\n</blockquote><p>\nThe B mandates were applied to the former <a href="/wiki/German_colonial_empire" title="German colonial empire">German colonies</a> that the League took responsibility for after the First World War. These were described as "peoples" that the League said were </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...at such a stage that the Mandatory must be responsible for the administration of the territory under conditions which will guarantee freedom of conscience and religion, subject only to the maintenance of public order and morals, the prohibition of abuses such as the slave trade, the arms traffic and the liquor traffic, and the prevention of the establishment of fortifications or military and naval bases and of military training of the natives for other than police purposes and the defence of territory, and will also secure equal opportunities for the trade and commerce of other Members of the League.<sup id="cite_ref-Mandate_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandate-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Article 22, The Covenant of the League of Nations</cite></div>\n</blockquote><p>\nSouth West Africa and certain South Pacific Islands were administered by League members under C mandates. These were classified as "territories" </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...which, owing to the sparseness of their population, or their small size, or their remoteness from the centres of civilisation, or their geographical contiguity to the territory of the Mandatory, and other circumstances, can be best administered under the laws of the Mandatory as integral portions of its territory, subject to the safeguards above mentioned in the interests of the indigenous population."<sup id="cite_ref-Mandate_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandate-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Article 22, The Covenant of the League of Nations</cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mandatory_powers">Mandatory powers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mandatory powers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The territories were governed by mandatory powers, such as the United Kingdom in the case of the <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Mandate of Palestine</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> in the case of South West Africa, until the territories were deemed capable of self-government.
