Fourteen mandate territories were divided up among seven mandatory powers: the United Kingdom, the Union of South Africa, France, Belgium, New Zealand, Australia and Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986194\xe2\x80\x93195_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986194\xe2\x80\x93195-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> With the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq" title="Kingdom of Iraq">Kingdom of Iraq</a>, which joined the League on 3 October 1932,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986216_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986216-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> these territories did not begin to gain their independence until after the Second World War, in a process that did not end until 1990. Following the demise of the League, most of the remaining mandates became <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Trust_Territories" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Trust Territories">United Nations Trust Territories</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the mandates, the League itself governed the <a href="/wiki/Saar_(League_of_Nations)" class="mw-redirect" title="Saar (League of Nations)">Territory of the Saar Basin</a> for 15 years, before it was returned to Germany following a plebiscite, and the <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Danzig" title="Free City of Danzig">Free City of Danzig</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Gda%C5%84sk" title="Gda\xc5\x84sk">Gda\xc5\x84sk</a>, Poland) from 15 November 1920 to 1 September 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198673\xe2\x80\x9375_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198673\xe2\x80\x9375-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Resolving_territorial_disputes">Resolving territorial disputes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Resolving territorial disputes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_World_War_I" title="Aftermath of World War I">aftermath of the First World War</a> left many issues to be settled, including the exact position of national boundaries and which country particular regions would join. Most of these questions were handled by the victorious Allied powers in bodies such as the Allied Supreme Council. The Allies tended to refer only particularly difficult matters to the League. This meant that, during the early <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">interwar period</a>, the League played little part in resolving the turmoil resulting from the war. The questions the League considered in its early years included those designated by the Paris Peace treaties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198670\xe2\x80\x9372_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198670\xe2\x80\x9372-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As the League developed, its role expanded, and by the middle of the 1920s it had become the centre of international activity. This change can be seen in the relationship between the League and non-members. The United States and Russia, for example, increasingly worked with the League. During the second half of the 1920s, France, Britain and Germany were all using the League of Nations as the focus of their diplomatic activity, and each of their foreign secretaries attended League meetings at Geneva during this period. They also used the League\'s machinery to try to improve relations and settle their differences.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenig1973170._115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenig1973170.-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id=".C3.85land_Islands"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="\xc3\x85land_Islands">\xc3\x85land Islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: \xc3\x85land Islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x85land crisis">\xc3\x85land crisis</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/%C3%85land" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x85land">\xc3\x85land</a> is a collection of around 6,500 islands in the Baltic Sea, midway between <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>. The islands are almost exclusively <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>-speaking, but in 1809, the \xc3\x85land Islands, along with Finland, were taken by <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Imperial Russia</a>. In December 1917, during the turmoil of the Russian <a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">October Revolution</a>, Finland declared its independence, but most of the \xc3\x85landers wished to rejoin Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197360_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197360-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The Finnish government considered the islands to be a part of their new nation, as the Russians had included \xc3\x85land in the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">Grand Duchy of Finland</a>, formed in 1809. By 1920, the dispute had escalated to the point that there was danger of war. The British government referred the problem to the League\'s Council, but Finland would not let the League intervene, as they considered it an internal matter. The League created a small panel to decide if it should investigate the matter and, with an affirmative response, a neutral commission was created.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197360_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197360-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In June 1921, the League announced its decision: the islands were to remain a part of Finland, but with guaranteed protection of the islanders, including demilitarisation. With Sweden\'s reluctant agreement, this became the first European international agreement concluded directly through the League.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198677\xe2\x80\x9378_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198677\xe2\x80\x9378-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_Silesia">Upper Silesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Upper Silesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Allied powers referred the problem of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Silesia" title="Upper Silesia">Upper Silesia</a> to the League after they had been unable to resolve the territorial dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197382\xe2\x80\x9383_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197382\xe2\x80\x9383-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> After the First World War, <a href="/wiki/Second_Polish_Republic" title="Second Polish Republic">Poland</a> laid claim to Upper Silesia, which had been part of <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>. The Treaty of Versailles had recommended a <a href="/wiki/Upper_Silesia_plebiscite" title="Upper Silesia plebiscite">plebiscite in Upper Silesia</a> to determine whether the territory should become part of Germany or Poland. Complaints about the attitude of the German authorities led to rioting and eventually to the first two <a href="/wiki/Silesian_Uprisings" title="Silesian Uprisings">Silesian Uprisings</a> (1919 and 1920). A plebiscite took place on 20 March 1921, with 59.6 percent (around 500,000) of the votes cast in favour of joining Germany, but Poland claimed the conditions surrounding it had been unfair. This result led to the <a href="/wiki/Third_Silesian_Uprising" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Silesian Uprising">Third Silesian Uprising</a> in 1921.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsmanczykMango20022568_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsmanczykMango20022568-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 12 August 1921, the League was asked to settle the matter; the Council created a commission with representatives from Belgium, Brazil, China and Spain to study the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198688_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198688-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The committee recommended that Upper Silesia be divided between Poland and Germany according to the preferences shown in the plebiscite and that the two sides should decide the details of the interaction between the two areas \xe2\x80\x93 for example, whether goods should pass freely over the border due to the economic and industrial interdependence of the two areas.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197383_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197383-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In November 1921, a conference was held in Geneva to negotiate a convention between Germany and Poland. A final settlement was reached, after five meetings, in which most of the area was given to Germany, but with the Polish section containing the majority of the region\'s mineral resources and much of its industry. When this agreement became public in May 1922, bitter resentment was expressed in Germany, but the treaty was still ratified by both countries. The settlement produced peace in the area until the beginning of the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198688_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198688-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Albania">Albania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Albania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The frontiers of the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Albania" title="Principality of Albania">Principality of Albania</a> had not been set during the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Conference,_1919" title="Paris Peace Conference, 1919">Paris Peace Conference</a> in 1919, as they were left for the League to decide; they had not yet been determined by September 1921, creating an unstable situation. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Greece_(Gl%C3%BCcksburg)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Greece (Gl\xc3\xbccksburg)">Greek</a> troops conducted military operations in the south of Albania. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Serbs,_Croats_and_Slovenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Serbs, Croats and Slovenes">Kingdom of Serbs, Croats and Slovenes</a> (Yugoslav) forces became engaged, after clashes with Albanian tribesmen, in the northern part of the country. The League sent a commission of representatives from various powers to the region. In November 1921, the League decided that the frontiers of Albania should be the same as they had been in 1913, with three minor changes that favoured Yugoslavia. Yugoslav forces withdrew a few weeks later, albeit under protest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986103\xe2\x80\x93105_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986103\xe2\x80\x93105-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The borders of Albania again became the cause of international conflict when Italian General <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Tellini" title="Enrico Tellini">Enrico Tellini</a> and four of his assistants were ambushed and killed on 24 August 1923 while marking out the newly decided border between Greece and Albania. Italian leader <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> was incensed, and demanded that a commission investigate the incident within five days. Whatever the results of the investigation, Mussolini insisted that the Greek government pay Italy fifty million <a href="/wiki/Italian_lira" title="Italian lira">lire</a> in reparations. The Greeks said they would not pay unless it was proved that the crime was committed by Greeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197386_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197386-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Mussolini sent a warship to shell the Greek island of <a href="/wiki/Corfu" title="Corfu">Corfu</a>, and Italian forces <a href="/wiki/Corfu_incident" title="Corfu incident">occupied the island</a> on 31 August 1923. This contravened the League\'s covenant, so Greece appealed to the League to deal with the situation. The Allies agreed (at Mussolini\'s insistence) that the <a href="/wiki/Conference_of_Ambassadors" title="Conference of Ambassadors">Conference of Ambassadors</a> should be responsible for resolving the dispute because it was the conference that had appointed General Tellini. The League Council examined the dispute, but then passed on their findings to the Conference of Ambassadors to make the final decision. The conference accepted most of the League\'s recommendations, forcing Greece to pay fifty million lire to Italy, even though those who committed the crime were never discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197387_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197387-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Italian forces then withdrew from Corfu.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986110_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986110-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memel">Memel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Memel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da_Revolt" title="Klaip\xc4\x97da Revolt">Klaip\xc4\x97da Revolt</a></div>\n<p>The port city of <a href="/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da" title="Klaip\xc4\x97da">Memel</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da" title="Klaip\xc4\x97da">Klaip\xc4\x97da</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da_Region" title="Klaip\xc4\x97da Region">the surrounding area</a>, with a predominantly German population, was under provisional Allied control according to Article 99 of the Treaty of Versailles. The French and Polish governments favoured turning Memel into an <a href="/wiki/International_city" title="International city">international city</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> wanted to annex the area. By 1923, the fate of the area had still not been decided, prompting Lithuanian forces to invade in January 1923 and seize the port. After the Allies failed to reach an agreement with Lithuania, they referred the matter to the League of Nations. In December 1923, the League Council appointed a Commission of Inquiry. The commission chose to cede Memel to Lithuania and give the area autonomous rights. The <a href="/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da_Convention" title="Klaip\xc4\x97da Convention">Klaip\xc4\x97da Convention</a> was approved by the League Council on 14 March 1924, and then by the Allied powers and Lithuania.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986107_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986107-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In 1939 Germany retook the region following the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazis</a> and an <a href="/wiki/1939_German_ultimatum_to_Lithuania" title="1939 German ultimatum to Lithuania">ultimatum to Lithuania</a>, demanding the return of the region under threat of war. The League of Nations failed to prevent the secession of the Memel region to Germany.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hatay">Hatay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hatay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hatay_State" title="Hatay State">Hatay State</a></div>\n<p>With League oversight, the <a href="/wiki/Sanjak" title="Sanjak">Sanjak</a> of <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0skenderun" title="\xc4\xb0skenderun">Alexandretta</a> in the <a href="/wiki/French_Mandate_of_Syria_and_the_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="French Mandate of Syria and the Lebanon">French Mandate of Syria</a> was given autonomy in 1937. Renamed Hatay, its parliament declared independence as the <a href="/wiki/Hatay_State" title="Hatay State">Republic of Hatay</a> in September 1938, after elections the previous month. It was annexed by <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> with French consent in mid-1939.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mosul">Mosul</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Mosul">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mosul_Question" title="Mosul Question">Mosul Question</a></div>\n<p>The League resolved a dispute between the Kingdom of Iraq and the Republic of Turkey over control of the former Ottoman province of <a href="/wiki/Mosul_Vilayet" title="Mosul Vilayet">Mosul</a> in 1926. According to the British, who had been awarded a League of Nations <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq_(Mandate_administration)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Iraq (Mandate administration)">mandate over Iraq</a> in 1920 and therefore represented Iraq in its foreign affairs, Mosul belonged to Iraq; on the other hand, the new Turkish republic claimed the province as part of its historic heartland. A League of Nations Commission of Inquiry, with Belgian, Hungarian and Swedish members, was sent to the region in 1924; it found that the people of Mosul did not want to be part of either Turkey or Iraq, but if they had to choose, they would pick Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973133_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973133-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In 1925, the commission recommended that the region stay part of Iraq, under the condition that the British hold the mandate over Iraq for another 25 years, to ensure the autonomous rights of the <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> population. The League Council adopted the recommendation and decided on 16 December 1925 to award Mosul to Iraq. Although Turkey had accepted League of Nations\' arbitration in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne_(1923)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Lausanne (1923)">Treaty of Lausanne (1923)</a>, it rejected the decision, questioning the Council\'s authority. The matter was referred to the Permanent Court of International Justice, which ruled that, when the Council made a unanimous decision, it must be accepted. Nonetheless, Britain, Iraq and Turkey ratified a separate treaty on 5 June 1926 that mostly followed the decision of the League Council and also assigned Mosul to Iraq. It was agreed that Iraq could still apply for League membership within 25 years and that the mandate would end upon its admission.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986107\xe2\x80\x93108_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986107\xe2\x80\x93108-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973131\xe2\x80\x93135_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973131\xe2\x80\x93135-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vilnius">Vilnius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Vilnius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/%C5%BBeligowski%27s_Mutiny" title="\xc5\xbbeligowski&#39;s Mutiny">\xc5\xbbeligowski\'s Mutiny</a></div>\n<p>After the First World War, Poland and Lithuania both regained their independence but soon became immersed in territorial disputes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198678_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198678-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Soviet_War" title="Polish\xe2\x80\x93Soviet War">Polish\xe2\x80\x93Soviet War</a>, Lithuania signed the <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Peace_Treaty" title="Soviet\xe2\x80\x93Lithuanian Peace Treaty">Moscow Peace Treaty</a> with the Soviet Union that laid out Lithuania\'s frontiers. This agreement gave Lithuanians control of the city of <a href="/wiki/Vilnius" title="Vilnius">Vilnius</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>: <i lang="lt">Vilnius</i>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>: <i lang="pl">Wilno</i>), the old Lithuanian capital, but a city with a majority Polish population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197361_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197361-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> This heightened tension between Lithuania and Poland and led to fears that they would resume the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_War" title="Polish\xe2\x80\x93Lithuanian War">Polish\xe2\x80\x93Lithuanian War</a>, and on 7 October 1920, the League negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Suwa%C5%82ki_Agreement" title="Suwa\xc5\x82ki Agreement">Suwa\xc5\x82ki Agreement</a> establishing a cease-fire and a demarcation line between the two nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198678_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198678-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> On 9 October 1920, General <a href="/wiki/Lucjan_%C5%BBeligowski" title="Lucjan \xc5\xbbeligowski">Lucjan \xc5\xbbeligowski</a>, commanding a Polish military force in contravention of the Suwa\xc5\x82ki Agreement, took the city and established the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Central_Lithuania" title="Republic of Central Lithuania">Republic of Central Lithuania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198678_131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198678-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a request for assistance from Lithuania, the League Council called for Poland\'s withdrawal from the area. The Polish government indicated they would comply, but instead reinforced the city with more Polish troops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197362_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197362-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> This prompted the League to decide that the future of Vilnius should be determined by its residents in a plebiscite and that the Polish forces should withdraw and be replaced by an international force organised by the League. The plan was met with resistance in Poland, Lithuania, and the Soviet Union, which opposed any international force in Lithuania. In March 1921, the League abandoned plans for the plebiscite.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott197363_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott197363-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> After unsuccessful proposals by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Hymans" title="Paul Hymans">Paul Hymans</a> to create a federation between Poland and Lithuania, Vilnius and the surrounding area was formally annexed by Poland in March 1922. After Lithuania took over the <a href="/wiki/Klaip%C4%97da_Region" title="Klaip\xc4\x97da Region">Klaip\xc4\x97da Region</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Conference_of_Ambassadors" title="Conference of Ambassadors">Allied Conference</a> set the frontier between Lithuania and Poland, leaving Vilnius within Poland, on 14 March 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198678\xe2\x80\x9379_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198678\xe2\x80\x9379-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Lithuanian authorities refused to accept the decision, and officially remained in a state of war with Poland until 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell200729_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell200729-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> It was not until the <a href="/wiki/1938_Polish_ultimatum_to_Lithuania" title="1938 Polish ultimatum to Lithuania">1938 Polish ultimatum</a> that Lithuania restored diplomatic relations with Poland and thus <i>de facto</i> accepted the borders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrampton199693_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrampton199693-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colombia_and_Peru">Colombia and Peru</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Colombia and Peru">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Colombia%E2%80%93Peru_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Colombia\xe2\x80\x93Peru War">Colombia\xe2\x80\x93Peru War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leticia_dispute" title="Leticia dispute">Leticia dispute</a></div>\n<p>There were several border conflicts between <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> in the early part of the 20th century, and in 1922, their governments signed the <a href="/wiki/Salom%C3%B3n-Lozano_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Salom\xc3\xb3n-Lozano Treaty">Salom\xc3\xb3n-Lozano Treaty</a> in an attempt to resolve them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOsmanczykMango20021314_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOsmanczykMango20021314-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> As part of this treaty, the border town of <a href="/wiki/Leticia,_Amazonas" title="Leticia, Amazonas">Leticia</a> and its surrounding area was ceded from Peru to Colombia, giving Colombia access to the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon River</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973249_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973249-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> On 1 September 1932, business leaders from Peruvian rubber and sugar industries who had lost land as a result organised an armed takeover of Leticia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethell1991414\xe2\x80\x93415_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethell1991414\xe2\x80\x93415-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> At first, the <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Peruvian government">Peruvian government</a> did not recognise the military takeover, but <a href="/wiki/President_of_Peru" title="President of Peru">President of Peru</a> <a href="/wiki/Luis_Miguel_S%C3%A1nchez_Cerro" title="Luis Miguel S\xc3\xa1nchez Cerro">Luis S\xc3\xa1nchez Cerro</a> decided to resist a Colombian re-occupation. The <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_Army" title="Peruvian Army">Peruvian Army</a> occupied Leticia, leading to an armed conflict between the two nations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973250_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973250-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> After months of diplomatic negotiations, the governments accepted mediation by the League of Nations, and their representatives presented their cases before the Council. A provisional peace agreement, signed by both parties in May 1933, provided for the League to assume control of the disputed territory while bilateral negotiations proceeded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973251_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973251-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In May 1934, a final peace agreement was signed, resulting in the return of Leticia to Colombia, a formal apology from Peru for the 1932 invasion, demilitarisation of the area around Leticia, free navigation on the Amazon and <a href="/wiki/Putumayo_River" title="Putumayo River">Putumayo Rivers</a>, and a pledge of <a href="/wiki/Non-aggression_pact" title="Non-aggression pact">non-aggression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saar">Saar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Saar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Saar_(League_of_Nations)" class="mw-redirect" title="Saar (League of Nations)">Saar</a> was a province formed from parts of Prussia and the <a href="/wiki/Palatinate_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Palatinate region">Rhenish Palatinate</a> and placed under League control by the Treaty of Versailles. A plebiscite was to be held after fifteen years of League rule to determine whether the province should belong to Germany or France. When the referendum was held in 1935, 90.3 percent of voters supported becoming part of Germany, which was quickly approved by the League Council.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198672\xe2\x80\x9373_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198672\xe2\x80\x9373-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChurchill198698_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChurchill198698-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_conflicts">Other conflicts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other conflicts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In addition to territorial disputes, the League also tried to intervene in other conflicts between and within nations. Among its successes were its fight against the international trade in opium and sexual slavery, and its work to alleviate the plight of refugees, particularly in Turkey in the period up to 1926. One of its innovations in this latter area was the 1922 introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Nansen_passport" title="Nansen passport">Nansen passport</a>, which was the first internationally recognised identity card for stateless refugees.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece_and_Bulgaria">Greece and Bulgaria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Greece and Bulgaria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Incident_at_Petrich" title="Incident at Petrich">Incident at Petrich</a></div>\n<p>After an incident involving sentries on the Greek-Bulgarian border in October 1925, fighting began between the two countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986112_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986112-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Three days after the initial incident, Greek troops invaded Bulgaria. The Bulgarian government ordered its troops to make only token resistance, and evacuated between ten thousand and fifteen thousand people from the border region, trusting the League to settle the dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973126\xe2\x80\x93127_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973126\xe2\x80\x93127-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> The League condemned the Greek invasion, and called for both Greek withdrawal and compensation to Bulgaria.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986112_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986112-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberia">Liberia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Liberia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Following accusations of forced labour on the large American-owned <a href="/wiki/Firestone_Tire_and_Rubber_Company" title="Firestone Tire and Rubber Company">Firestone</a> rubber plantation and American accusations of slave trading, the Liberian government asked the League to launch an investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiers2003140\xe2\x80\x93141_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiers2003140\xe2\x80\x93141-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The resulting commission was jointly appointed by the League, the United States, and Liberia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiers2003188_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiers2003188-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> In 1930, a League report confirmed the presence of slavery and forced labour. The report implicated many government officials in the selling of contract labour and recommended that they be replaced by Europeans or Americans, which generated anger within Liberia and led to the resignation of President <a href="/wiki/Charles_D._B._King" title="Charles D. B. King">Charles D. B. King</a> and his vice-president. The Liberian government outlawed forced labour and slavery and asked for American help in social reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiers2003188_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiers2003188-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mukden_Incident:_Japan_attacks_China">Mukden Incident: Japan attacks China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Mukden Incident: Japan attacks China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mukden_Incident" title="Mukden Incident">Mukden Incident</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sesi%C3%B3nDeLaSociedadDeNacionesSobreManchuria1932.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sesi%C3%B3nDeLaSociedadDeNacionesSobreManchuria1932.jpeg/220px-Sesi%C3%B3nDeLaSociedadDeNacionesSobreManchuria1932.jpeg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sesi%C3%B3nDeLaSociedadDeNacionesSobreManchuria1932.jpeg/330px-Sesi%C3%B3nDeLaSociedadDeNacionesSobreManchuria1932.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Sesi%C3%B3nDeLaSociedadDeNacionesSobreManchuria1932.jpeg/440px-Sesi%C3%B3nDeLaSociedadDeNacionesSobreManchuria1932.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1193" data-file-height="841" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sesi%C3%B3nDeLaSociedadDeNacionesSobreManchuria1932.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese delegate addresses the League of Nations concerning the <a href="/wiki/Mukden_Incident" title="Mukden Incident">Manchurian Crisis</a> in 1932.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Mukden Incident, also known as the "Manchurian Incident" was a decisive setback that weakened The League because its major members refused to tackle Japanese aggression. Japan itself withdrew.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the agreed terms of the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-One_Demands" title="Twenty-One Demands">Twenty-One Demands</a> with China, the Japanese government had the right to station its troops in the area around the <a href="/wiki/South_Manchurian_Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="South Manchurian Railway">South Manchurian Railway</a>, a major trade route between the two countries, in the Chinese region of <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>.  In September 1931, a section of the railway was lightly damaged by the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Kwantung_Army" title="Kwantung Army">Kwantung Army</a> as a pretext for an invasion of Manchuria.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIriye19878_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIriye19878-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENish1977176\xe2\x80\x93178_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENish1977176\xe2\x80\x93178-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The Japanese army claimed that Chinese soldiers had sabotaged the railway and in apparent retaliation (acting contrary to orders from Tokyo, <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973208_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973208-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>) occupied all of Manchuria. They renamed the area <a href="/wiki/Manchukuo" title="Manchukuo">Manchukuo</a>, and on 9 March 1932 set up a puppet government, with <a href="/wiki/Pu_Yi" class="mw-redirect" title="Pu Yi">Pu Yi</a>, the former emperor of China, as its executive head.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986139_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986139-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> This new entity was recognised only by the governments of Italy, Spain and Nazi Germany; the rest of the world still considered Manchuria legally part of China.\n</p><p>The League of Nations sent observers. The <a href="/wiki/Lytton_Report" title="Lytton Report">Lytton Report</a> appeared a year later (October 1932). It declared Japan to be the aggressor and demanded Manchuria be returned to China.  The report passed 42\xe2\x80\x931 in the Assembly in 1933 (only Japan voting against), but instead of removing its troops from China, Japan withdrew from the League.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986156\xe2\x80\x93161_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986156\xe2\x80\x93161-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In the end,  as British historian <a href="/wiki/Charles_Mowat" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Mowat">Charles Mowat</a> argued, collective security was dead:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>The League and the ideas of collective security and the rule of law were defeated; partly because of indifference and of sympathy with the aggressor, but partly because the League powers were unprepared, preoccupied with other matters, and too slow to perceive the scale of Japanese ambitions.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chaco_War">Chaco War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Chaco War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chaco_War" title="Chaco War">Chaco War</a></div>\n<p>The League failed to prevent the 1932 war between <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> over the arid <a href="/wiki/Gran_Chaco" title="Gran Chaco">Gran Chaco</a> region. Although the region was sparsely populated, it contained the <a href="/wiki/Paraguay_River" title="Paraguay River">Paraguay River</a>, which would have given either landlocked country access to the Atlantic Ocean,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973242\xe2\x80\x93243_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973242\xe2\x80\x93243-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> and there was also speculation, later proved incorrect, that the Chaco would be a rich source of petroleum.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELevy200121\xe2\x80\x9322_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevy200121\xe2\x80\x9322-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Border skirmishes throughout the late 1920s culminated in an all-out war in 1932 when the Bolivian army attacked the Paraguayans at Fort Carlos Antonio L\xc3\xb3pez at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Pitiantuta" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake Pitiantuta">Lake Pitiantuta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBethell1991495_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBethell1991495-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Paraguay appealed to the League of Nations, but the League did not take action when the <a href="/wiki/Pan-American_Conference" title="Pan-American Conference">Pan-American Conference</a> offered to mediate instead. The war was a disaster for both sides, causing 57,000 casualties for Bolivia, whose population was around three million, and 36,000 dead for Paraguay, whose population was approximately one million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973248_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973248-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> It also brought both countries to the brink of economic disaster. By the time a ceasefire was negotiated on 12 June 1935, Paraguay had seized control of most of the region, as was later recognised by the 1938 truce.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScheina2003103_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScheina2003103-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italian_invasion_of_Abyssinia">Italian invasion of Abyssinia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Italian invasion of Abyssinia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Abyssinia_Crisis" title="Abyssinia Crisis">Abyssinia Crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War">Second Italo-Abyssinian War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SelassieInJerusalem.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/SelassieInJerusalem.jpg/220px-SelassieInJerusalem.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/SelassieInJerusalem.jpg/330px-SelassieInJerusalem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/SelassieInJerusalem.jpg/440px-SelassieInJerusalem.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4333" data-file-height="3155" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SelassieInJerusalem.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emperor <a href="/wiki/Haile_Selassie_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Haile Selassie I">Haile Selassie</a> escaping Ethiopia via Jerusalem</div></div></div>\n<p>In October 1935, Italian dictator Benito Mussolini sent 400,000 troops to invade Abyssinia (<a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986222\xe2\x80\x93225_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986222\xe2\x80\x93225-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Marshal <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Badoglio" title="Pietro Badoglio">Pietro Badoglio</a> led the campaign from November 1935, ordering bombing, the use of chemical weapons such as <a href="/wiki/Mustard_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustard gas">mustard gas</a>, and the poisoning of water supplies, against targets which included undefended villages and medical facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986222\xe2\x80\x93225_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986222\xe2\x80\x93225-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHillGarvey1995629_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHillGarvey1995629-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The modern <a href="/wiki/Italian_Army" title="Italian Army">Italian Army</a> defeated the poorly armed Abyssinians and captured <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a> in May 1936, forcing Emperor of Ethiopia <a href="/wiki/Haile_Selassie_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Haile Selassie I">Haile Selassie</a> to flee.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986221_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986221-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The League of Nations condemned Italy\'s aggression and imposed economic sanctions in November 1935, but the sanctions were largely ineffective since they did not ban the sale of oil or close the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> (controlled by Britain).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaer1976245_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaer1976245-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>, the British Prime Minister, later observed, this was ultimately because no one had the military forces on hand to withstand an Italian attack.<sup id="cite_ref-events_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-events-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> In October 1935, the US President, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, invoked the recently passed <a href="/wiki/Neutrality_Acts_of_1930s" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutrality Acts of 1930s">Neutrality Acts</a> and placed an embargo on arms and munitions to both sides, but extended a further "moral embargo" to the belligerent Italians, including other trade items. On 5 October and later on 29 February 1936, the United States endeavoured, with limited success, to limit its exports of oil and other materials to normal peacetime levels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaer197671_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaer197671-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> The League sanctions were lifted on 4 July 1936, but by that point Italy had already gained control of the urban areas of Abyssinia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaer1976298_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaer1976298-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hoare%E2%80%93Laval_Pact" title="Hoare\xe2\x80\x93Laval Pact">Hoare\xe2\x80\x93Laval Pact</a> of December 1935 was an attempt by the British Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hoare,_1st_Viscount_Templewood" title="Samuel Hoare, 1st Viscount Templewood">Samuel Hoare</a> and the French Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Laval" title="Pierre Laval">Pierre Laval</a> to end the conflict in Abyssinia by proposing to partition the country into an Italian sector and an Abyssinian sector. Mussolini was prepared to agree to the pact, but news of the deal leaked out. Both the British and French public vehemently protested against it, describing it as a sell-out of Abyssinia. Hoare and Laval were forced to resign, and the British and French governments dissociated themselves from the two men.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaer1976121\xe2\x80\x93155_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaer1976121\xe2\x80\x93155-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> In June 1936, although there was no precedent for a head of state addressing the Assembly of the League of Nations in person, Haile Selassie spoke to the Assembly, appealing for its help in protecting his country.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Abyssinian crisis showed how the League could be influenced by the self-interest of its members;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaer1976303_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaer1976303-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> one of the reasons why the sanctions were not very harsh was that both Britain and France feared the prospect of driving Mussolini and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> into an alliance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaer197677_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaer197677-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Civil_War">Spanish Civil War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Spanish Civil War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a></div>\n<p>On 17 July 1936, the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Army" title="Spanish Army">Spanish Army</a> launched a coup d\'\xc3\xa9tat, leading to a prolonged armed conflict between Spanish <a href="/wiki/Second_Spanish_Republic" title="Second Spanish Republic">Republicans</a> (the elected leftist national government) and the Nationalists (conservative, anti-communist rebels who included most officers of the Spanish Army).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELannon200225\xe2\x80\x9329_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELannon200225\xe2\x80\x9329-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julio_%C3%81lvarez_del_Vayo" title="Julio \xc3\x81lvarez del Vayo">Julio \xc3\x81lvarez del Vayo</a>, the Spanish Minister of Foreign Affairs, appealed to the League in September 1936 for arms to defend Spain\'s territorial integrity and political independence. The League members would not intervene in the Spanish Civil War nor prevent foreign intervention in the conflict. <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> and Mussolini continued to aid General <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a>\'s Nationalists, while the Soviet Union helped the Spanish Republic. In February 1937, the League did ban <a href="/wiki/International_Brigades" title="International Brigades">foreign volunteers</a>, but this was in practice a symbolic move.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986264\xe2\x80\x93265,_269\xe2\x80\x93270_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986264\xe2\x80\x93265,_269\xe2\x80\x93270-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Sino-Japanese_War">Second Sino-Japanese War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Second Sino-Japanese War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a></div>\n<p>Following a long record of instigating localised conflicts throughout the 1930s, Japan began a full-scale invasion of China on 7 July 1937. On 12 September, the Chinese representative, <a href="/wiki/Wellington_Koo" title="Wellington Koo">Wellington Koo</a>, appealed to the League for international intervention. Western countries were sympathetic to the Chinese in their struggle, particularly in their stubborn <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shanghai" title="Battle of Shanghai">defence of Shanghai</a>, a city with a substantial number of foreigners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986270_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986270-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The League was unable to provide any practical measures; on 4 October, it turned the case over to the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Power_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Nine Power Treaty">Nine Power Treaty Conference</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Failure_of_disarmament">Failure of disarmament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Failure of disarmament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/World_Disarmament_Conference" title="World Disarmament Conference">World Disarmament Conference</a></div>\n<p>Article 8 of the Covenant gave the League the task of reducing "armaments to the lowest point consistent with national safety and the enforcement by common action of international obligations".<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> A significant amount of the League\'s time and energy was devoted to this goal, even though many member governments were uncertain that such extensive disarmament could be achieved or was even desirable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986113,_123_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986113,_123-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The Allied powers were also under obligation by the Treaty of Versailles to attempt to disarm, and the armament restrictions imposed on the defeated countries had been described as the first step toward worldwide disarmament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986113,_123_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986113,_123-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> The League Covenant assigned the League the task of creating a disarmament plan for each state, but the Council devolved this responsibility to a special commission set up in 1926 to prepare for the 1932\xe2\x80\x931934 <a href="/wiki/World_Disarmament_Conference" title="World Disarmament Conference">World Disarmament Conference</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986114_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986114-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Members of the League held different views towards the issue. The French were reluctant to reduce their armaments without a guarantee of military help if they were attacked; Poland and <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> felt vulnerable to attack from the west and wanted the League\'s response to aggression against its members to be strengthened before they disarmed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenig1973173_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenig1973173-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Without this guarantee, they would not reduce armaments because they felt the risk of attack from Germany was too great. Fear of attack increased as Germany regained its strength after the First World War, especially after Adolf Hitler gained power and became <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler%27s_rise_to_power" title="Adolf Hitler&#39;s rise to power">German Chancellor in 1933</a>. In particular, Germany\'s attempts to overturn the Treaty of Versailles and the reconstruction of the German military made France increasingly unwilling to disarm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986114_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986114-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Disarmament_Conference" title="World Disarmament Conference">World Disarmament Conference</a> was convened by the League of Nations in Geneva in 1932, with representatives from 60 states. It was a failure.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> A one-year moratorium on the expansion of armaments, later extended by a few months, was proposed at the start of the conference.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldblat200224_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldblat200224-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> The Disarmament Commission obtained initial agreement from France, Italy, Spain, Japan, and Britain to limit the size of their navies but no final agrfeement was reached.  Ultimately, the Commission failed to halt the military build-up by Germany, Italy, Spain and Japan during the 1930s. \n</p><p>The League was mostly silent in the face of major events leading to the Second World War, such as Hitler\'s <a href="/wiki/Remilitarisation_of_the_Rhineland" class="mw-redirect" title="Remilitarisation of the Rhineland">remilitarisation of the Rhineland</a>, occupation of the <a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenland</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, which had been forbidden by the Treaty of Versailles. In fact, League members themselves re-armed. In 1933, Japan simply withdrew from the League rather than submit to its judgement,<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> as did Germany the same year (using the failure of the World Disarmament Conference to agree to arms parity between France and Germany as a pretext), Italy and Spain in 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge198647,_133_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge198647,_133-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> The final significant act of the League was to expel the Soviet Union in December 1939 after <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">it invaded Finland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986273_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986273-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_weaknesses">General weaknesses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: General weaknesses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Gap_in_the_Bridge.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/The_Gap_in_the_Bridge.png/220px-The_Gap_in_the_Bridge.png" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/The_Gap_in_the_Bridge.png/330px-The_Gap_in_the_Bridge.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/The_Gap_in_the_Bridge.png/440px-The_Gap_in_the_Bridge.png 2x" data-file-width="2584" data-file-height="1829" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Gap_in_the_Bridge.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>The Gap in the Bridge</b>; the sign reads "This League of Nations Bridge was designed by the President of the U.S.A." Cartoon from <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i> magazine, 10 December 1920, satirising the gap left by the US not joining the League.</div></div></div>\n<p>The onset of the Second World War demonstrated that the League had failed in its primary purpose, the prevention of another world war. There were a variety of reasons for this failure, many connected to general weaknesses within the organisation. Additionally, the power of the League was limited by the United States\' refusal to join.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986276\xe2\x80\x93278_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986276\xe2\x80\x93278-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_structure">Origins and structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Origins and structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The origins of the League as an organisation created by the Allied powers as part of the peace settlement to end the First World War led to it being viewed as a "League of Victors".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGorodetsky199426_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGorodetsky199426-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaffo19741_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaffo19741-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> The League\'s neutrality tended to manifest itself as indecision. It required a unanimous vote of nine, later fifteen, Council members to enact a resolution; hence, conclusive and effective action was difficult, if not impossible. It was also slow in coming to its decisions, as certain ones required the unanimous consent of the entire Assembly. This problem mainly stemmed from the fact that the primary members of the League of Nations were not willing to accept the possibility of their fate being decided by other countries, and by enforcing unanimous voting had effectively given themselves veto power.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986279\xe2\x80\x93282,_288\xe2\x80\x93292_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986279\xe2\x80\x93282,_288\xe2\x80\x93292-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_representation">Global representation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Global representation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Representation at the League was often a problem. Though it was intended to encompass all nations, many never joined, or their period of membership was short. The most conspicuous absentee was the United States. President Woodrow Wilson had been a driving force behind the League\'s formation and strongly influenced the form it took, but the US Senate voted not to join on 19 November 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnock1995263_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnock1995263-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Henig,_Baroness_Henig" title="Ruth Henig, Baroness Henig">Ruth Henig</a> has suggested that, had the United States become a member, it would have also provided support to France and Britain, possibly making France feel more secure, and so encouraging France and Britain to co-operate more fully regarding Germany, thus making the rise to power of the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> less likely.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenig1973175_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenig1973175-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Conversely, Henig acknowledges that if the US had been a member, its reluctance to engage in war with European states or to enact economic sanctions might have hampered the ability of the League to deal with <a href="/wiki/International_incident" title="International incident">international incidents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenig1973175_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenig1973175-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> The structure of the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">US federal government</a> might also have made its membership problematic, as its representatives at the League could not have made decisions on behalf of the <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">executive branch</a> without having the prior approval of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">legislative branch</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenig1973176_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenig1973176-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 1920, when the League was born, Germany was not permitted to join because it was seen as having been the aggressor in the First World War. <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">Soviet Russia</a> was also initially excluded, as Communist regimes were not welcomed. The League was further weakened when major powers left in the 1930s. Japan began as a permanent member of the Council, but withdrew in 1933 after the League voiced opposition to its occupation of Manchuria.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDonough199762_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDonough199762-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Italy began as a permanent member of the Council, but withdrew in 1937. Spain also began as a permanent member of the Council, but withdrew in 1939. The League had accepted Germany, also as a permanent member of the Council, in 1926, deeming it a "peace-loving country", but Adolf Hitler pulled Germany out when he came to power in 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDonough199769_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDonough199769-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collective_security">Collective security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Collective security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Another important weakness grew from the contradiction between the idea of <a href="/wiki/Collective_security" title="Collective security">collective security</a> that formed the basis of the League and <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relations</a> between individual states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986253_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986253-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> The League\'s collective security system required nations to act, if necessary, against states they considered friendly, and in a way that might endanger their <a href="/wiki/National_interest" title="National interest">national interests</a>, to support states for which they had no normal affinity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986253_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986253-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> This weakness was exposed during the <a href="/wiki/Abyssinia_Crisis" title="Abyssinia Crisis">Abyssinia Crisis</a>, when Britain and France had to balance maintaining the security they had attempted to create for themselves in Europe "to defend against the enemies of internal order",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986254_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986254-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> in which Italy\'s support played a pivotal role, with their obligations to Abyssinia as a member of the League.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986253\xe2\x80\x93254_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986253\xe2\x80\x93254-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nOn 23 June 1936, in the wake of the collapse of League efforts to restrain Italy\'s war against Abyssinia, the British Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>, told the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> that collective security had </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>failed ultimately because of the reluctance of nearly all the nations in Europe to proceed to what I might call military sanctions ... The real reason, or the main reason, was that we discovered in the process of weeks that there was no country except the aggressor country which was ready for war ... [I]f collective action is to be a reality and not merely a thing to be talked about, it means not only that every country is to be ready for war; but must be ready to go to war at once. That is a terrible thing, but it is an essential part of collective security.<sup id="cite_ref-events_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-events-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Ultimately, Britain and France both abandoned the concept of collective security in favour of <a href="/wiki/Appeasement" title="Appeasement">appeasement</a> in the face of growing German militarism under Hitler.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDonough199774_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDonough199774-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\nIn this context, the League of Nations was also the institution where the first international debate on terrorism took place following the 1934 assassination of <a href="/wiki/King_Alexander_I_of_Yugoslavia" class="mw-redirect" title="King Alexander I of Yugoslavia">King Alexander I of Yugoslavia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseille</a>, showing its conspiratorial features, many of which are detectable in the discourse of terrorism among states after <a href="/wiki/9/11" class="mw-redirect" title="9/11">9/11</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>American diplomatic historian <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Flagg_Bemis" title="Samuel Flagg Bemis">Samuel Flagg Bemis</a> originally supported the League,  but after two decades changed his mind:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>The League of Nations has been a disappointing failure.... It has been a failure, not because the United States did not join it; but because the great powers have been unwilling to apply sanctions except where it suited their individual national interests to do so, and because Democracy, on which the original concepts of the League rested for support, has collapsed over half the world.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacifism_and_disarmament">Pacifism and disarmament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Pacifism and disarmament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The League of Nations lacked an armed force of its own and depended on the Great Powers to enforce its resolutions, which they were very unwilling to do.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDonough199754\xe2\x80\x935_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDonough199754\xe2\x80\x935-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Its two most important members, Britain and France, were reluctant to use sanctions and even more reluctant to resort to military action on behalf of the League. Immediately after the First World War, <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifism</a> became a strong force among both the people and governments of the two countries. The <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">British Conservatives</a> were especially tepid to the League and preferred, when in government, to negotiate treaties without the involvement of that organisation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986238\xe2\x80\x93240_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986238\xe2\x80\x93240-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, the League\'s advocacy of disarmament for Britain, France, and its other members, while at the same time advocating collective security, meant that the League was depriving itself of the only forceful means by which it could uphold its authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986134\xe2\x80\x93135_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986134\xe2\x80\x93135-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When the British cabinet discussed the concept of the League during the First World War, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Hankey,_1st_Baron_Hankey" title="Maurice Hankey, 1st Baron Hankey">Maurice Hankey</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_Secretary" title="Cabinet Secretary">Cabinet Secretary</a>, circulated a memorandum on the subject. He started by saying, "Generally it appears to me that any such scheme is dangerous to us, because it will create a sense of security which is wholly fictitious".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett1972245_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett1972245-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> He attacked the British pre-war faith in the sanctity of treaties as delusional and concluded by claiming:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It [a League of Nations] will only result in failure and the longer that failure is postponed the more certain it is that this country will have been lulled to sleep. It will put a very strong lever into the hands of the well-meaning idealists who are to be found in almost every Government, who deprecate expenditure on armaments, and, in the course of time, it will almost certainly result in this country being caught at a disadvantage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett1972245_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett1972245-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The Foreign Office minister Sir <a href="/wiki/Eyre_Crowe" title="Eyre Crowe">Eyre Crowe</a> also wrote a memorandum to the British cabinet claiming that "a solemn league and covenant" would just be "a treaty, like other treaties". "What is there to ensure that it will not, like other treaties, be broken?" Crowe went on to express scepticism of the planned "pledge of common action" against aggressors because he believed the actions of individual states would still be determined by national interests and the balance of power. He also criticised the proposal for League economic sanctions because it would be ineffectual and that "It is all a question of real military preponderance". Universal disarmament was a practical impossibility, Crowe warned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett1972245_208-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett1972245-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demise_and_legacy">Demise and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=League_of_Nations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Demise and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:18-IV-1946.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/18-IV-1946.png/220px-18-IV-1946.png" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/18-IV-1946.png/330px-18-IV-1946.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/18-IV-1946.png/440px-18-IV-1946.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="868" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:18-IV-1946.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World map showing <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_the_League_of_Nations" title="Member states of the League of Nations">member states of the League of Nations</a> (in green and red) on 18 April 1946, when the League of Nations ceased to exist.</div></div></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Humanit%C3%A9s_Num%C3%A9riques.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Humanit%C3%A9s_Num%C3%A9riques.JPG/220px-Humanit%C3%A9s_Num%C3%A9riques.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Humanit%C3%A9s_Num%C3%A9riques.JPG/330px-Humanit%C3%A9s_Num%C3%A9riques.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Humanit%C3%A9s_Num%C3%A9riques.JPG/440px-Humanit%C3%A9s_Num%C3%A9riques.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Humanit%C3%A9s_Num%C3%A9riques.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>League of Nations archives, Geneva.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>As the situation in Europe escalated into war, the Assembly transferred enough power to the Secretary General on 30 September 1938 and 14 December 1939 to allow the League to continue to exist legally and carry on reduced operations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagliveras199931_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagliveras199931-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The headquarters of the League, the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Nations" title="Palace of Nations">Palace of Nations</a>, remained unoccupied for nearly six years until the Second World War ended.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973399_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973399-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the 1943 <a href="/wiki/Tehran_Conference" title="Tehran Conference">Tehran Conference</a>, the Allied powers agreed to create a new body to replace the League: the United Nations. Many League bodies, such as the International Labour Organisation, continued to function and eventually became affiliated with the UN.<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The designers of the structures of the United Nations intended to make it more effective than the League.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986278\xe2\x80\x93280_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorthedge1986278\xe2\x80\x93280-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The final meeting of the League of Nations took place on 18 April 1946 in Geneva.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Delegates from 34 nations attended the assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973404_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973404-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> This session concerned itself with liquidating the League: it transferred assets worth approximately $22,000,000 (U.S.) in 1946<sup id="cite_ref-SyracuseHerald_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SyracuseHerald-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> (including the Palace of Nations and the League\'s archives) to the UN, returned reserve funds to the nations that had supplied them, and settled the debts of the League.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott1973404_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott1973404-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_1st_Viscount_Cecil_of_Chelwood" title="Robert Cecil, 1st Viscount Cecil of Chelwood">Robert Cecil</a>, addressing the final session, said:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Let us boldly state that aggression wherever it occurs and however it may be defended, is an international crime, that it is the duty of every peace-loving state to resent it and employ whatever force is necessary to crush it, that the machinery of the Charter, no less than the machinery of the Covenant, is sufficient for this purpose if properly used, and that every well-disposed citizen of every state should be ready to undergo any sacrifice in order to maintain peace ... I venture to impress upon my hearers that the great work of peace is resting not only on the narrow interests of our own nations, but even more on those great principles of right and wrong which nations, like individuals, depend.<br />\n</p><p>The League is dead.
